  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6b2806ce-45ab-417e-92dd-2bdc062176e1'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer8278873584764615363">BELIEVING IS DANGEROUS...<br/><br/>Darcy Patel is afraid to believe all the hype. But it's really happening - her teen novel is getting published.   <span id="freeText8278873584764615363" style="display:none">BELIEVING IS DANGEROUS...<br /><br />Darcy Patel is afraid to believe all the hype. But it's really happening - her teen novel is getting published. Instead of heading to college, she's living in New York City, where she's welcomed into the dazzling world of YA publishing. That means book tours, parties with her favorite authors, and finding a place to live that won't leave her penniless. It means sleepless nights rewriting her first draft and struggling to find the perfect ending... all while dealing with the intoxicating, terrifying experience of falling in love - with another writer.<br /><br />Told in alternating chapters is Darcy's novel, the thrilling story of Lizzie, who wills her way into the afterworld to survive a deadly terrorist attack. With survival comes the responsibility to guide the restless spirits that walk our world, including one ghost with whom she shares a surprising personal connection. But Lizzie's not alone in her new calling - she has counsel from a fellow spirit guide, a very desirable one, who is torn between wanting Lizzie and warning her that...<br /><br />BELIEVING IS DANGEROUS.<br /><br />In a brilliant high-wire act of weaving two epic narratives - and two unforgettable heroines - into one novel, Scott Westerfeld's latest work is a triumph of storytelling.</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/116555-i-don-t-know-much-but-i-m-preeeeeeety">I don&#39;t know much, but I&#39;m PREEEEEEETY sure a publisher would take more than a few weeks to read a manuscript and sign a contract. From what I know of the publishing world, it&#39;s more like months. Am I the only one who thinks that?</a>
</div>

<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_116555'><a id="like_count_community_question_116555" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/116555?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18367581-afterworlds">like</a></span>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>

</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It takes a publisher a while sometimes, but for an agent, it can happen very fast. Her agent signed a contract with her very quickly in the book. Afte<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It takes a publisher a while sometimes, but for an agent, it can happen very fast. Her agent signed a contract with her very quickly in the book. After that, the agent wrangles with editors at publishing houses, and hammering out the contract can take a while, especially if it's a big deal like Darcy's. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3003455-jenn-one-of-many-we-are-legion?shelf=quirky">quirky</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3003455-jenn-one-of-many-we-are-legion?shelf=edelweiss">edelweiss</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3003455-jenn-one-of-many-we-are-legion?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3003455-jenn-one-of-many-we-are-legion?shelf=reviews-to-transfer">reviews-to-transfer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3003455-jenn-one-of-many-we-are-legion?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer932902937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14879157175733885819">So, I kinda loved this book, its intelligence, its meta-awareness, and the way a lovely coming-of-age story is packaged inside a clever satire-bordering-on-love-story to the YA publishing world. It must be said that I love satire, even when it's subdued, because that love colors my entire opinion of this book.<br/><br/>As such, I’m baffled by the somewhat low reviews on this one. I could be completely off-base, and maybe I am seeing what I want to see because I want an excuse for liking this book as much </span>
  <span id="freeText14879157175733885819" style="display:none">So, I kinda loved this book, its intelligence, its meta-awareness, and the way a lovely coming-of-age story is packaged inside a clever satire-bordering-on-love-story to the YA publishing world. It must be said that I love satire, even when it's subdued, because that love colors my entire opinion of this book.<br /><br />As such, I’m baffled by the somewhat low reviews on this one. I could be completely off-base, and maybe I am seeing what I want to see because I want an excuse for liking this book as much as I did, but since this is my review, I’m going to use this review space to tell you why I think everyone else is wrong and you should bow to my opinion. <br /><br />First, a little background.<br /><br /><b>The Plot:</b> This book actually contains two books, neither of which can be fully realized without the other (more on that below). The main story revolves around Darcy. Darcy is 18, Indian, and, as she later learns, a lesbian. Borrowing secrets from her mother’s past and her parents’ religion (Hinduism), she wrote a manuscript during NanoWrimo when she was a senior in high school, and to everyone’s surprise, publishers loved it and gave her a fat, two-book publishing deal. She has almost a year to submit her final revisions before publication. Her story is a contemporary coming-of-age tale as she decides to defer college and live her dream by moving to New York to live as a novelist by immersing herself in the YA writing world.<br /><br />The second story, entitled <i>Afterworlds</i>, is the novel that Darcy actually wrote. This story is presumably in its final form and is a fairly typical paranormal romance. Its main plot centers around Lizzie, who, after surviving a traumatic event, becomes a reaper, or psychopomp (note - that’s a real word, not one made up for the plot), whose purpose is to help guide the souls/ghosts of the recently deceased to the Afterworlds.<br /><br />The two stories alternate chapters, and the brilliance of this is in seeing how the two stories intertwine. More specifically, the brilliance in this choice is seen in how the events in Darcy’s chapters affect the story that she writes and how she appropriates all her life experiences into the book’s narrative.<br /><br />On to the review/condemnation of other reviews. I will put these under individual spoiler tags for length. <i>Note, I am not disclosing any actual spoilers under the tags, other than a couple cute quotes and moments that don't spoil the plot.</i><br /><br /><b>This isn’t how publishing works!</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Frankly, I’m going to err on the side of the author who has traditionally published numerous books over those who have not been involved in the traditional publishing world for years. That’s just me. Yes, many writers, especially the young, work hard and never make it. Because writing is not easy. But many do make it, and many become overnight juggernauts. That some manuscripts never see the light of day doesn't mean this story isn't realistic - it means they aren't as talented or lucky as the protag in this book.<br /><br />And from the things I have looked up, the publishing underbelly exposed in Darcy’s chapters seems to reflect traditional pub reality. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>But she’s only 17 when she gets a deal! That never happens! This is just wish fulfillment! <i>So</i> unrealistic!</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Ok, there are three things here. First, as the author is a 50-something year-old man who has written and traditionally published numerous novels to great success, there is nothing wish fulfilling about this. His wishes came true long ago, so that’s just a false comparison. Second, it <b>does</b> happen. Veronica Roth, anyone? Anna Todd?<br /><br />Third, let’s talk a moment about super-special MCs. I get that we don’t want someone unrealistically special in every read. But do you really want to read the tale of Harry Potter from, say, Pansy Parkinson’s perspective? No? There’s a reason for that. It’s because it would bore you. If there’s nothing special about our main character at all, why do we want to read about them? You don’t want to read a book about how your neighbor lives their mundane life because you engage in the mundane every day. So the fact that Darcy happens to be within a small percentage of people for whom traditional publishing not only becomes a reality, but also while she is very young, should not be a deterrent to reading the book. As a main character, there has to be something compelling enough about her that you want to read it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>But this book make it sound so easy and demeans the hard work of writers everywhere!</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Hogwash. Writing comes more easily for some than others. Some are luckier than others. That is fact. Some people are prolifically talented. Some people have words and stories overflowing in their brains. I know one GR author who routinely writes new works in a very short period of time. Besides, this book in no way makes Darcy’s job seem easy. Sure, the initial manuscript was picked up quickly, but it took no time at all for her to learn it was nowhere near publication ready, and she spends the next year of her life struggling to make it so.<br /><br />During that year, she even learns how unprepared she was for all of this - she learns that people <i>pretended</i>to laugh at her jokes and love her manuscript, but the truth is, she has a long road left to haul.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Lizzie’s story is so typical. I can’t believe Scott Westerfeld wrote this!</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Her story <i>is</i> typical, but that’s actually the point. <b>Am I the only one sees the heavy satire in this story?</b> It’s so blatant that I can’t understand how no one else has mentioned it, or how Lizzie’s story is used to justify disliking the book. Full disclosure - Lizzie’s story is full of some tropes that some readers dislike, but I am convinced these cliches are intentional and used to drive the run that is the main narrative. It’s used for Westerfeld to write his love letter about the industry, and in his letter, he addresses its pitfalls.<br /><br />Delightfully meta and self-aware, the main narrative routinely pokes fun at the YA writing world, including its use of common tropes. Beginning on page 2, as Darcy describes her query letter, she explains to the agent how <i>"her novel was different from the other paranormals of the last few years."</i> Convinced that her manuscript is special, we then get to read its first chapter in which Lizzie meets a reaper in the Afterworld during the middle of a terrorist attack. You know what Lizzie’s first thought is? You got it - she waxes about his beauty and they kiss.<br /><br />More examples: When Darcy later attends an industry party, and is teased for writing a PNR, because those are <i>so over,</i> she expresses her belief that there's a million ways to tell a love story, "especially when it's love with someone who's different." C'mon, that's funny! Can no one else see that Westerfeld is making jokes about his own profession? He knew fully well when he wrote that line that not only were the characters no different at all, but that they <i>all</i> think their book is different.<br /><br />As though to hammer home the point, and get in a little shade about book snobbery, someone else pays her the backhanded compliment of telling Darcy that her book is better than "the average paranormal.” It was a fun way to express how much smoke is blown up an authors ass by all their supporters (agent, family, friends, publisher, etc), as though to exhibit that Darcy, despite being special, really ain't so special. <br /><br />But see, this is where the book really starts to get good, because Westerfeld basically deconstructs the genre through Darcy’s chapters, and using what she learns in the industry, we get to see how her experiences color her re-writes. <br /><br />At one point, for instance, Darcy is basically accused of using her parents’ Hindu god for “purposes of YA hotness;” she readily admits that she modeled him after a Bollywood actor and model because "he's the hot guy who was never in the PNRs she read when she was little...but she didn't want it to be about <i>her</i> wanting him." On the very next page, she mentions why she chose for him to speak English, despite him being a Hindu god, because she didn't want them to have to "mime their undying love." She also admits that she is inexperienced writing about a character traumatized by a terrorist attack. <br /><br />Now go back to Lizzie and see its effects in action. She is immediately entranced by his beauty and kisses him within minutes of meeting him, despite being on the verge of death during a freaking terrorist attack. She writes these scenes with the sheen of someone in love, but never gives that relationship the development it needs or deserves. Darcy inability to understanding how to write such a character explains why Lizzie is flat and has virtually no negative reaction to the event. <br /><br /><b><s>Westerfeld</s> Darcy has blatantly admitted to Mary Sue-ing her protag.</b> That's quite telling. And that fact is not lost on Westerfeld or Darcy, as she later writes a passage in her novel in Lizzie tries to convince her best friend that they were “connected” from first sight. The best friend isn’t buying it: “<i>By which you mean, he’s hot.”</i><br /><br />Perhaps the most telling line is this one: "Everyone loved that first chapter, but it had made all the ones after it a lot trickier." Go read some of the reviews for this book. Count how many times people talk about the first Lizzie chapter being great, but then talk about subsequent chapters falling flat or being cliched. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br /><b>Reality check: this book is so fucking meta it doesn't even know how meta it is.</b><br /><br />Other fun tidbits about the biz that evidence Westerfeld’s intent to satirize (but not to the point of parody) his livelihood:<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Virtually everyone in the YA writers’ circle is writing a trilogy. They speak of YA authors frequently borrowing from Jane Austen. A ghost in Lizzie’s story goes out of her way to describe how her actions are in no way creepy or stalkery. The reapers? Are shiny. Kinda sparkly, in fact. No joke, at one point Lizzie actually says that she does not like “cold, wet things.” Nightly discussions about world-building. Darcy wonders how to fit the word Panopticon into her novel. There are nods to the “strong” heroine who randomly gets saved by the love interest. The series that just won’t die. Love triangle jokes. Who is to blame when a book doesn’t sell - the cover artist, marketing, the author, the title? Funny exchanges discussing how The Printz Award is actually better than being knighted. <b>The importance of luck and timing in a book hitting its sales marks.</b><br /><br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />How anyone else isn't seeing this as subversive satire is beyond me. There’s an entire character in this book that’s so obviously John Green he might as well have been wearing a blue "Okay? Okay" t-shirt in all his scenes. <br /><br />Even the publisher’s name - Paradox - fits with Westerfeld’s overall themes. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ He subtly hints a few times that there are certain tropes and plot devices that are fairly industry standard (the immortal who looks 17, for instance), because that’s what the masses want. Yet, when the masses receive what it is they want - what sells - they shred it:<br /><br /><blockquote>Exactly! They <i>want</i> the genre tropes, but they can’t <i>handle</i> the genre tropes!</blockquote><br /><br />It’s a paradox the writer can’t win, as evidenced, in my opinion, by the reviews on this very site. <br /><br />Westerfeld even offers commentary on all the things it that reviewers will attack him for in the novel. <b>He pokes fun at his own readers, for crying out loud.</b> Westerfeld’s critique of his own novel within a novel is rather ingenious. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>There’s so much swearing! Who talks this way!? What lazy dialogue.</b> Wrong.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ One review actually says “if we are to believe current writers” this is how people talk, but they aren’t buying it because no one actually says “Fuck” that much. Sister, you aren’t hanging out with a lot of Gen-Xers and younger, then, because this has been going on since the 90s. In fact, <b>the dialogue might be some of the most realistic I’ve seen in the genre.</b> One such line is, “what the actual fuck?” I’ve said that. Have you? That’s what I thought.<br /><br />Loosening language standards aren’t limited to millennials; this has been going on since Generation X really started to come of age. Look at any trend created or made famous by a Gen Xer or younger (South Park, which is an adult show, btw, is an excellent example) and you get an idea of how people really speak. <i>Look at the reviews on this very website, many of which are articulate and smart discourses, written by millennials, but peppered with colorful language. .</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I find it frustrating when who aren’t surrounded by the culture just write off a very visible and obvious part of the culture as a trend, as though the author wrote that way as a ploy for attention. That simply is not the case. This dialogue is realistic enough that it could have been me having a conversation with any one of you. This isn’t a trend - this is how people actually speak these days. <br /><br />The same can be said for the fact that the lead character is a lesbian. That’s not the writer seeking attention, it’s not a damn trend, there are many people who happen to be lesbians, and the author is simply reflecting that reality by making his lead reflect reality. That’s to say nothing of the fact that YA PNRs typically have a Caucasian female lead and are almost exclusively heterosexual - points that Westerfeld subtly touches upon in Darcy’s chapters. <br /><br />(As an aside, this same review also claimed it’s unrealistic that any teenager would even know what a First Edition is, much less have reading copies - uhhh, one of my good friends collected FEs from the time she was a teenager, and I recall her devastation after they were destroyed during a natural disaster. So I find this particular person’s perspective on what is realistic is bit dubious, to say the least)<br /><br /><b>He isn’t exploiting trends - he is using trends to make commentary.</b> There is a difference and it again brings me back to the whole, <i>how does not one else see the obvious satirical elements at work here?</i> <br /><br /><b>Pfffffft, c’mon! Navigating into adulthood isn’t this easy! She found an apartment in, like, a day!</b> That brings us to Darcy’s coming-of-age tale. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ She's never lived away from home before and now she has loads of money and intends to live in NYC. Like most young people who leave home, she has ideas on the type of "group" she wants to fit into, and she works to make it true. She thinks she belongs with other writers, wants to find writerly clothes to wear, and live in an inspirational, hip, writerly neighborhood. Like many young people, she doesn’t know who she is yet, so in the process of figuring it out, she tries to become who she wants to be. <br /><br />Also like many young people leaving home, she is convinced that this will be easy. Her sister created a budget for her, but a cursory glance at it reveals how woefully unprepared she is and life is about to smack her in the face. When she arrives in New York, she does find an apartment within a day, but only after spending the entire day looking (and it doesn’t hurt that she spends way over budget). I’ve lived in a big city - it only takes a day if you’re diligent and have a decent budget, so honestly that’s a false criticism.<br /><br />More to the point, however, is that Darcy finds that growing up and becoming an adult, living within a budget, doing and buying the mundane everyday things that grown-ups must so, isn’t easy. She never had to think about buying a broom before. She thought she could easily pay her bills on $17.00 per day. She thought she would be whisked away into YA fantasy land and live happily ever after, that her romance would be like those in the novels, never to end or have problems. But none of that actually happens, as she learns the hard way.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>In the midst of it all, though, he has a great rolling commentary about cultural appropriation. </b> Westerfeld is even satirical in his dialogue about cultural appropriation - I don't think it's an accident that due to the color of her skin, Darcy is "allowed" to use "her" culture, despite that she was raised as an American and has zero links to the culture of her parents' homeland (also, her parents don’t seem to be terribly religious themselves). I think he's making a legitimate criticism about the over-sensitivity some seem to have <s>about flipping <i>everything</i></s>, and how hypocritical it is that Darcy can appropriate, but others cannot, just because of their skin color. That is, just because she's genetically Indian doesn't mean she's not appropriating. <br /><br />Don't get me wrong, however, he treats the subject with the seriousness it deserves, he really does. Although he pokes a little fun, as he does throughout the book on its myriad subjects, he also presents different viewpoints designed to let you think on the subject and draw your own conclusions.<br /><br />Ultimately, I loved this one. It’s smart, funny, has a lot to say about the reading world, and has some very fun bits in there about a debutante publishee navigating through the world of adulthood and newbie publishing. The PNR within the book is not nearly as enjoyable, but that seems an intentional choice to help drive home Darcy’s story and growth, as well as to enhance Westerfeld’s sociocultural commentary.<br /><br /><b>5 stars for Darcy's story, but only a 3 for Lizzie's, which, while necessary and important for Darcy, was lackluster enough to drive down the overall rating.</b><br /><br />Thank you to Simon Pulse via Edelweiss for providing me with a complimentary copy of this book for review. All quotes are taken from an uncorrected galley subject to change before publishing.</span>

<div id="review_974895830" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/974895830" />
    <a title="Kat Heckenbach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2712862-kat-heckenbach"><img alt="Kat Heckenbach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490743030p2/2712862.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/974895830?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat Heckenbach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat Heckenbach" href="/user/show/2712862-kat-heckenbach">Kat Heckenbach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer974895830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18137231657829562436">I'm going to start this out with a plea that someone please tell me this book is a parody. It *must* be. Because I am a huge fan of Scott Westerfeld's writing, have been for years, and this book makes me want to cry :(.<br/><br/>It is a dual story line. The main character, Darcy, is an 18 yr old, just graduated from high school girl who had written a novel in a month, in November of course, not that NaNoWriMo is specifically mentioned, but any writer can put two and two together. She sent off her draft to</span>
  <span id="freeText18137231657829562436" style="display:none">I'm going to start this out with a plea that someone please tell me this book is a parody. It *must* be. Because I am a huge fan of Scott Westerfeld's writing, have been for years, and this book makes me want to cry :(.<br><br>It is a dual story line. The main character, Darcy, is an 18 yr old, just graduated from high school girl who had written a novel in a month, in November of course, not that NaNoWriMo is specifically mentioned, but any writer can put two and two together. She sent off her draft to a huge literary agent, got signed, immediately got signed with a publishing deal with an advance of several hundred thousand dollars, and then intermittently whines about how long it will take her manuscript to actually be published. She moves to New York, where her mega-agent allows her to borrow her apartment until she's able to find one of her own. The mega-agent's apartment is of course practically a mansion inside. And Darcy of course finds the "perfect" apartment in a single day of looking. Party, party, party, meet lots of cool, hip YA authors who do nothing but talk about their own books, and of course, there is an insta-love relationship between Darcy and one of the authors she meets.<br><br>The other story line is the actual book that Darcy wrote. It's mildly interesting, but slow. The first chapter was pretty killer, then it kinda nose-dived for me. It's a paranormal about a girl who can "cross over" into the afterlife and can see ghosts after a near-death experience at an airport under attack by terrorists. (Enter the other insta-love relationship.) I felt completely unconnected to her. I don't care how tough you are, you have issues when you see that kind of stuff, but Lizzie is just fine. Her mom is a complete moron. Seriously--please tell me this is a parody....<br><br>Westerfeld's characters in all his other books have been fully fleshed and interesting, but I did not find them so in this one. All the writers, as I mentioned, are two-dimensional and self-absorbed. There is so much writer speak thrown in--which seems like it would bore the pants off a non-writer, as being a writer myself I started getting tired of it. I really began to wonder--is this how the world sees writers? Or worse, it this how we see each other?<br><br>The writing often feels dumbed-down, while the characters all act years older than they really are. Naive at times, yes, but far too witty and mature with their dialog and habits. Also, Darcy is supposed to be Indian, and it seemed a bit of a contrivance that her family decided to reject their culture and Americanize. An easy way to feature a non-white character and be able to write her like a typical white American.<br><br>The thing that really bugged me though, and I haven't seen this mentioned in any other reviews--but surely someone has to have noticed--so much time is spent with people telling Darcy how amazing her book/writing is. Well, Darcy is not real, which means that Darcy's "book" was written by Westerfeld himself. So, his own made-up characters are raving about a book *he* wrote --&gt; in other words, he spends a great amount of time essentially telling himself how wonderful his story is. Again, if this isn't a parody, it's total narcissism.<br><br>Please, please, someone tell me this is a parody so I can stop hating it.<br><br>**adding an amendment. That last point I made--let me clarify. I don't know SW and am not calling him a narcissist. But the way this book is written, it gives that *appearance.* The way the other characters gush over Darcy's books lays it out as though the readers have to believe that the other part of the story is like the best-freaking-thing ever written in YA. <br><br>There is a series by Cornelia Funke (Inkheart) in which the characters are in love with a book (also Inkheart), and eventually get to enter the story world of that book. But there are only a few passages actually quoted, it's more about the characters and setting, and the "book" inside the book is one that was not successful. It's more like you're reading about a very specific and niche group of fans of this one book, which completely negates any narcissistic feel. That, however, is lacking in Afterworlds. <br><br><a href="http://www.katheckenbach.com" rel="nofollow noopener">My Website</a><br><a href="https://www.facebook.com/kat.heckenbach" rel="nofollow noopener">Find me on Facebook</a><br>My YA fantasy series:<br>book 1<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25325662.Finding_Angel__Toch_Island_Chronicles___1_" title="Finding Angel (Toch Island Chronicles, #1) by Kat Heckenbach" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1428558696s/25325662.jpg" alt="Finding Angel (Toch Island Chronicles, #1) by Kat Heckenbach" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>book 2<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16000213.Seeking_Unseen__Toch_Island_Chronicles___2_" title="Seeking Unseen (Toch Island Chronicles, #2) by Kat Heckenbach" rel="nofollow noopener">

<div id="review_1031670840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1031670840" />
    <a title="Wendy Darling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling"><img alt="Wendy Darling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379959930p2/2745288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1031670840?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wendy Darling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wendy Darling" href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling">Wendy Darling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=2014-publication">2014-publication</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=to-review">to-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=action-packed">action-packed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=poc">poc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=lgbtqi">lgbtqi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=pn-reapers">pn-reapers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=own-dtb-tbr">own-dtb-tbr</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=male-ya-author">male-ya-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1031670840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13215507412638593856"><b>3.5 stars</b> I really enjoyed reading this. Despite its 600 pages, both Darcy's and Lizzie's alternating narratives flew by, and I enjoyed perching on the young debut author's shoulder as she navigates the writing and publishing world. The author's humorous, razor-sharp observations of YA culture are fascinating and feel familiar to anyone who's on Twitter, attended industry events, or even been present at business gatherings where there's frantic ennui and kindly condescension in the ranks, though</span>
  <span id="freeText13215507412638593856" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars</b> I really enjoyed reading this. Despite its 600 pages, both Darcy's and Lizzie's alternating narratives flew by, and I enjoyed perching on the young debut author's shoulder as she navigates the writing and publishing world. The author's humorous, razor-sharp observations of YA culture are fascinating and feel familiar to anyone who's on Twitter, attended industry events, or even been present at business gatherings where there's frantic ennui and kindly condescension in the ranks, though I wonder how interesting that will be to those who aren't somewhat invested in publishing.<br /><br />I was also surprised to find that Darcy the YA author's story arc, particularly her pre-pub debut year and her first grown-up relationship, was the one I responded to more strongly. Lizzie's paranormal plot has a fantastic opening chapter (so meta that this is mentioned in Darcy's story as well) and is unexpectedly creepy, but I wanted a bit more from the ending than what we got, and I was never really all that convinced by the romance in that timeline.<br /><br />Still, a compelling read, and one I'd recommend. A bit more of a review to come at some point.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13215507412638593856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_888236730" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/888236730" />
    <a title="Brigid ✩" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/886144-brigid"><img alt="Brigid ✩" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581869052p2/886144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/888236730?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brigid ✩" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brigid ✩" href="/user/show/886144-brigid">Brigid ✩</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=arcs">arcs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=ghosts">ghosts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer888236730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9100318767210256030"><b>After reading:</b><br/><br/>It like physically pains me to say this, but ... I found this book disappointing. Look ... I love love love Scott Westerfeld. Like, so much. He's one of my favorite authors in the world and I recommend his books to people constantly. I love <i>Uglies</i> and <i>Midnighters</i> and goodness gracious I adore the <i>Leviathan</i> books and couldn't wait to see what Scott Westerfeld would do next. So naturally, I was excited for this book and I was ecstatic to receive an ARC––but honestly, it didn't live u</span>
  <span id="freeText9100318767210256030" style="display:none"><b>After reading:</b><br><br>It like physically pains me to say this, but ... I found this book disappointing. Look ... I love love love Scott Westerfeld. Like, so much. He's one of my favorite authors in the world and I recommend his books to people constantly. I love <i>Uglies</i> and <i>Midnighters</i> and goodness gracious I adore the <i>Leviathan</i> books and couldn't wait to see what Scott Westerfeld would do next. So naturally, I was excited for this book and I was ecstatic to receive an ARC––but honestly, it didn't live up to my expectations. Don't get me wrong, it's a fairly entertaining/addicting read and even though it's 600 pages it didn't feel too long to me. And there was some potential in the idea, particularly in the inclusion of Hindu mythology (which I've only seen very occasionally in YA books––or in any book, really)––but even that wasn't fleshed out all that much.<br><br>Over all, the whole dual-story thing didn't really work for me and I didn't really see what the point of it was. Both of the main characters were pretty dry and I didn't feel much for them ... in fact, Lizzie was kind of a horrible person but I'll get more into that when I write my full review. And mostly I just found myself really annoyed at Darcy's storyline––partly because I dislike reading about writers (which is weird because I am one, but ... maybe that's why) but also writing/publishing were portrayed <i>so</i> unrealistically in this book and I had a hard time suspending my disbelief about a lot of things.<br><br>... But once again, I'll go into more detail when I write an actual, full review of this book. So yeah, you can expect that some time in the near future. <br><br><br><b>Before reading:</b><br><br>OKAY SO. I went to BookExpo America and I managed to get my hands on an ARC of this! (It involved waiting in line for like an hour, but there was free beer and I met some cool librarian/reviewer people so it was a good time.) And then I actually met Scott Westerfeld and he signed the book so that was super exciting because I've wanted to meet him forever and he's one of my favorite authors in the world so AAAHHHH. And––get this––he said he was pretty sure he'd heard of me ... ?! Like, he knew about my review blog and stuff ... ! I just. I. WHAT. I like pretty much screamed "<i>REALLY?</i>" and probably made a total idiot of myself. Oh my goodness, what a beautiful moment. I'm never going to get over it. But anyway, here it is ... the book! It's beautiful! And HUGE.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1401559146i/9818489._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1401559146i/9818490._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'm still not really sure how I feel about this story-within-a-story thing––and from what I've read so far the whole teenage-girl-publishing-her-book storyline is super unrealistic (as I kind of feared it would be)––but the other storyline (the plot of the girl's book) is intriguing so far. And over all I'm excited to see where this whole idea is going because right now I have no clue. This is the 14th Scott Westerfeld book I've read (if I'm not forgetting anything) and he's never let me down so yeah I have some pretty high expectations. ;)<br><br>-------------<br>-------------<br><br><b>Before reading:</b><br><br>WHAT WHAT WHAT. A NEW SCOTT WESTERFELD BOOK YOU SAY?!?! COMING OUT IN THE FALL?!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1395461960ra/9000570.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Ahhh I've been wondering when there would be a new Westerfeld book. I've read all his other YA books and loved all of them. The <i>Leviathan, Uglies, </i>and <i>Midnighters</i> trilogies are among my favorite series, and I'm always eager to see what he'll write next.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1395461960ra/9000571.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>According to <a href="http://publishersweekly.com/pw/by-topic/childrens/childrens-book-news/article/61421-uglies-author-westerfeld-shines-a-light-on-the-publishing-industry-for-next-ya-novel.html" rel="nofollow noopener">this article</a> on the Publishers Weekly website:<br><br><blockquote>The story follows Darcy Patel to New York City after she lands a publishing contract for a novel she wrote for NaNoWriMo (National Novel Writing Month) during her senior year of high school. As she becomes acquainted with the city’s charms and pitfalls, Darcy revises her manuscript and learns to navigate the publishing process along the way. ...<br><br>In addition to providing an insider’s look at the publishing industry, Afterworlds offers a second entire plotline: relayed in alternating chapters is Darcy’s book-in-progress, a paranormal thriller about a teenager named Lizzie who survives a terrorist attack by thinking her way into a mysterious “afterworld” between life and death. While in this limbo she embarks on myriad adventures, acting as a guide for lost souls and unsettled ghosts.</blockquote><br><br>Hmmmm. I mean ... part of me just kind of wishes the book would just be about that second plotline because that sounds SO COOL but the publishing part sounds ... ehhh. I'm not a huge fan of stories about writing/publishing ... but I don't know, hopefully it'll still be interesting.<br><br>Also, at least this is going to be a standalone which means no waiting for months for a sequel! Hooray!<br><br>So yeah, sounds kind of different from his usual stuff but I can't wait to read it. <br><br><b>Update 3/31/14:</b> Oooh there's a cover. Hmm ... it's simple, but I like it. Kind of surreal/mysterious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9100318767210256030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1915817141834614188">Darcy is a YA novelist, fresh out of high school and deferring college (indefinitely?) because she <i>sold her first novel to a publisher within a few weeks of sending a query</i>. <b>So this is clearly a work of fiction</b>.<br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1425143130ra/13852762.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I didn’t care much for her book, with the premise being a girl who can see ghosts and falls in love with a Hindu death god. The passages where we see chapters from the book are dull, to be honest but I can see how they would have sold big in the book market (at least the way it was a few </span>
  <span id="freeText1915817141834614188" style="display:none">Darcy is a YA novelist, fresh out of high school and deferring college (indefinitely?) because she <i>sold her first novel to a publisher within a few weeks of sending a query</i>. <b>So this is clearly a work of fiction</b>.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1425143130ra/13852762.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I didn’t care much for her book, with the premise being a girl who can see ghosts and falls in love with a Hindu death god. The passages where we see chapters from the book are dull, to be honest but I can see how they would have sold big in the book market (at least the way it was a few years ago).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1431538363i/14844923._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>But there’s another story here, one of Darcy adulting around New York; of her navigating new territory that she hadn’t given much thought to.<br><br>The author was trying to make several points in this book, but for whatever reason I wasn’t really feeling it.<br></span>

<div id="review_969986783" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/969986783" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/969986783?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/202724-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=youngadult">youngadult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer969986783" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer234638088965702267">This is a big, fascinating, ambitious book, and if anyone could pull something like this off you'd think it would be Westerfeld. But . . . I was really a little shocked at the things that just didn't work for me. I wondered if it was just me, but a quick scan of other readers shows similar reactions. <br/><br/>So, back up, let me explain.<br/><br/>This is not one book, it's two. <br/><br/>The odd-numbered chapters are about a girl named Darcy Patel who wrote a book for NaNoWriMo (National Novel Writing Month, which is Novemb</span>
  <span id="freeText234638088965702267" style="display:none">This is a big, fascinating, ambitious book, and if anyone could pull something like this off you'd think it would be Westerfeld. But . . . I was really a little shocked at the things that just didn't work for me. I wondered if it was just me, but a quick scan of other readers shows similar reactions. <br /><br />So, back up, let me explain.<br /><br />This is not one book, it's two. <br /><br />The odd-numbered chapters are about a girl named Darcy Patel who wrote a book for NaNoWriMo (National Novel Writing Month, which is November, if you aren't in the know), actually sold it, and uses her advance money to move to New York and be an author. The even-numbered chapters are bordered in black and are Darcy's novel, which is about a girl named Lizzie who has a near-death experience and can now see the dead. So, pretty cool, right? And the novel-in-the-novel (entitled Afterworlds, of all things) is at times very creepy, and there is a hot romance, and it actually could have stood alone as a book.<br /><br />But I almost wish it hadn't. Part of Darcy's story is that she's dealing with working with an editor for the first time. Freaking out over changes suggested to her ending, worrying that she's used the word "veins" too many times, that she's commandeered parts of her parents' Hindu faith for the sake of hot YA romance. I kept waiting to see changes to Afterworlds, like a character suddenly having an accent, or even reading the same chapter twice, but now it ends differently. But halfway through I realized that the Lizzie chapters were already edited, and what we were seeing were the final, polished, product. Which sort of robbed the book of some of its impact, for me anyway. Now I was just reading a straight up YA paranormal romance, which isn't really my thing. Also, Darcy freaks out over the fact that the copyeditor can find almost no physical descriptions for the characters in the books, and I agree. I couldn't picture most of the people (but the settings are great and very creepy) in Afterworlds, and didn't care that much about them by the end. Also, one of the characters is an eleven-year-old girl. It's a good thing that they keep saying her age, because from her behavior I would have pegged her as being six. It became increasingly grating to read about someone who acted like a six-year-old but kept stating her age as eleven. Did no one in or outside of the book notice this? Have any of them ever spoken to an eleven-year-old?<br /><br />So the novel-in-a-novel was a bit disappointing, in that I would have loved to have seen the editorial process. And the novel-outside-of-the-novel, Darcy's story, was a bit . . . well . . . I loved some of it, and some of it annoyed me. I loved that she wrote and sold her NaNoWriMo novel. I loved that her sister, a math genius, figured out her budget so that she could move to New York and be a writer. That's the dream, right? I still have that dream! I loved her reactions to the publishing world, finding out about book tours, about editing, about sales numbers and all the nitty gritty that no one tells you about. <br /><br />But then it was like Westerfeld pulled his punches. Yes, she's really seeing what's inside the writing world. But she's not failing, she's hitting one streak of awesome luck after another. She sells her novel in less than a year. Her agent lets her stay in her fancy New York apartment while she finds a place of her own. She finds an amazing apartment with awesome views in one day of looking. She falls in love. Even though she's repeatedly saying she's way over her budget, she just keeps going, shopping and eating out with her new friends. Despite her worries and her writer's block, her book is still getting great buzz. I just kept waiting for it to all come crashing down. And I couldn't believe that she was getting all this, at eighteen. Sure, maybe it was jealousy on my part . . . but still. It was like she never struggled with anything for more than five minutes. Her publisher has no qualms about throwing an eighteen-year-old out into the world of publishing, or that she can't follow through and do edits and turn out a second book in less than a year. She worries about breaking the news to her parents that she's not going to college next year, but they accept it. She puts off telling them about her relationship, since her partner (who pretty much moves in after one date) is FIVE YEARS OLDER THAN SHE IS AND SHE IS BARELY LEGAL (Sorry, sorry, Mom-instinct), and her parents smile and say, How wonderful you're in love! She drinks beer bought with her fake ID and never gets caught. The adults around her buy her drinks and see her in a relationship with an older, wiser person and living on her own in New York fresh out of high school and are like, Isn't this amazing? Yes, it's amazing, it's also, in a way, more of a fantasy than the story of Lizzie walking among the dead! It's also highly problematic in terms of audience: While "Afterworlds" is totally suitable for teens, Darcy's exterior story is . . . really pushing the envelope. She's a high school grad, eighteen years old, in a relationship with a twenty-something. There's plenty of profanity, as in, copious amounts of the f-word, underage drinking, and Darcy pretty much living as an adult in an adult world. I would hesitate to give this to a teen, just because of that. <br /><br />So, to sum up, and I really didn't mean to get into this so much, but . . . I loved the idea of this book. I loved the insider look at publishing, which I do recommend (with a grain of salt) to those interested in publishing. But at the same time I just need you to remember: This is fiction. Also, swearing is bad.</span>
  <a data-text-id="234638088965702267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_949785045" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/949785045" />
    <a title="Heidi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1208981-heidi"><img alt="Heidi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1213629596p2/1208981.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/949785045?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heidi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heidi" href="/user/show/1208981-heidi">Heidi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1208981-heidi?shelf=vine">vine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1208981-heidi?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1208981-heidi?shelf=nyc">nyc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1208981-heidi?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer949785045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14795631121702387117">I love this times infinity. I already love YA in general and Scott Westerfeld in particular (his <em>Uglies</em> series got me back into reading YA as an adult), but this book I love the most. I love that he wrote something so chancy and cheeky (and META!), and I love that someone published it. Basically I think it's brilliant and couldn't love it more, but I don't think everyone is going to feel that way. <br/><br/>Here's some of what I love and some of what sets this book apart, if you want to know if it's for y</span>
  <span id="freeText14795631121702387117" style="display:none">I love this times infinity. I already love YA in general and Scott Westerfeld in particular (his <em>Uglies</em> series got me back into reading YA as an adult), but this book I love the most. I love that he wrote something so chancy and cheeky (and META!), and I love that someone published it. Basically I think it's brilliant and couldn't love it more, but I don't think everyone is going to feel that way. <br /><br />Here's some of what I love and some of what sets this book apart, if you want to know if it's for you:<br /><br />1. I wasn't kidding about the meta. Not only is this a YA book about someone writing a YA book, but the YA book she's writing is also included (in alternating chapters). There's also a lot of discussion about the challenges of writing (and publishing, and marketing, and selling) YA, some of which has already surfaced in the reviews of <em>this</em> book (one of the main story's characters - another YA author - is worried that her protagonist, a girl who likes girls who like to set fires, is too risky and won't be well received; lo and behold, several of the <em>Afterworlds</em> reviews I've read contain warnings about its violence and "homosexual relationship"). So while Westerfeld is writing about a fictitious writer and her fictitious book, he's also exploring some very real issues that very real YA writers face.<br /><br />2. One of the most interesting of those issues is the matter of cultural appropriation. Darcy, the main character of the main story (the one writing the book within the book), has some misgivings about one of <em>her</em> main characters, who is based on a Hindu death god. She worries that she is exploiting her religion (one she doesn't really observe) "for the purposes of YA hotness" (the scary death god has been turned into a handsome love interest). I love all the discussions about this, and I especially love that this teenage gay Indian girl fretting over cultural appropriation is written by a fifty-something straight white man. Some people might take issue with that, but this book is so gloriously self-reflective that it hardly matters. It's kind of the point. (Also mad props to Westerfeld for whipping out Millenialisms like "squee" and "this is giving me all the feelings.")<br /><br />3. There's a lot of focus on the YA writing/publishing community, particularly in New York. I enjoyed this because I live in New York and I live to read YA. Like Darcy I moved to NYC alone, young and clueless and ended up in an exorbitantly overpriced 4th floor walk-up in Chinatown (though instead of a $150K publishing deal I received $150K in grad school debt). The notion of a teenager landing said deal might rub some aspiring writers the wrong way, but it's what allows Westerfeld to acquaint us with his world in such detail. We learn and experience everything right alongside Darcy. This probably won't interest everybody. I've heard grumblings that Darcy's part of the story is unnecessary and that Lizzie's story (the book within the book) should have been the whole shabang, but for me it's the intertwining of the two narratives that makes this so special. You don't need to be a writer to appreciate the writing process. What reader hasn't finished an amazing book and then wondered about the mind that produced it? <em>Afterworlds</em> is a chance to crawl inside that mind and see the whole thing unfold.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14795631121702387117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_862426795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/862426795" />
    <a title="Rashika (is tired)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8492956-rashika-is-tired"><img alt="Rashika (is tired)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476647072p2/8492956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/862426795?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rashika (is tired)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rashika (is tired)" href="/user/show/8492956-rashika-is-tired">Rashika (is tired)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=good-female-leads">good-female-leads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=loved">loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=good-secondary-characters">good-secondary-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=good-characters">good-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=e-arcs">e-arcs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=2014-favorites">2014-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=realistic-contemporary">realistic-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8492956-rashika-is-tired?shelf=mind-blown">mind-blown</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer862426795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13813945941074235190"><i>***This review has also been posted on <b><a href='http://wp.me/p3p0qg-WV' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Social Potato</a></b></i><br/><br/>I don’t know where to start with this book but I will say this, <strong>Scott Westerfeld is a genius.</strong> I love him to bits and pieces and this book reminds me why. When I first heard he had a new book coming out, I was over the moon, to the point where I didn’t pay much attention to the blurb just because I believed I’d love it and love it I did. This may not be his best work, and the novel may seem a little fluffy but there is so much more to it.<strong> It</strong></span>
  <span id="freeText13813945941074235190" style="display:none"><i>***This review has also been posted on <b><a href="http://wp.me/p3p0qg-WV" rel="nofollow noopener">The Social Potato</a></b></i><br /><br />I don’t know where to start with this book but I will say this, <strong>Scott Westerfeld is a genius.</strong> I love him to bits and pieces and this book reminds me why. When I first heard he had a new book coming out, I was over the moon, to the point where I didn’t pay much attention to the blurb just because I believed I’d love it and love it I did. This may not be his best work, and the novel may seem a little fluffy but there is so much more to it.<strong> It has so many hidden depths that come crawling out yet at the same time it makes you laugh.</strong> Who doesn’t love a book that can make you laugh yet at the same time makes you think?<br /><br /><em>Afterworlds</em>, to me, is a work of art. The story follows two different timelines, one of the creator and the other of the created. <strong>A book about a book! BOOKCEPTION!</strong> Darcy is a young author, barely out of high school who got paid a huge advance for her book and can finally be an adult (YAY, Adulting is fun. NOT). Lizzie is the main character of said book. You’d think the two storylines wouldn’t have much connecting them aside from the fact that Darcy made up Lizzie but that’s not true. <strong>What really connects them, under all of that, is the fact that both are coming of age stories. Both these young women are thrust into situations out of their depth and as a result they grow.</strong> Darcy becomes a highly paid (unprinted) author at a young age and has to learn how to fit into the publishing community. She's also a tad naive so a lot of her journey is also about realizing that not everything is 'YA heaven'. Lizzie on the other hand has to learn to deal with all the death surrounding her and has to also learn all about this whole new world she finds herself in.<br /><br />You know what’s brilliant though? <strong>I DON’T HAVE A FAVORITE TIMELINE!</strong> I loved both of them equally. There were times when I was more sucked in by one than the other but when I closed the book I didn’t have a favorite.<br /><br /><strong>Darcy’s storyline was the one that made me ask the questions though.</strong> The glimpse we got of the publishing community left me hyper (I really do mean hyper... like jumping off the walls hyper) and just hungry to know more. It was so fascinating and even a little scary to see what authors (may) go through. The amount of hard work they put in, the problems they may come across and those bits broke my heart.  Darcy herself was a wonderful, sometimes annoying but all around fun to read about character. I think what made her so realistic was that in spite of everything that had happened to her, <strong>she wasn’t a mature adult.</strong> She was out of her depth. She didn’t do things perfectly and carefully<strong>. She blew her budget, screwed things up but at the end of it all, she learned from her mistakes. She grew up. That’s what made her storyline beautiful.</strong><br /><br />Lizzie’s on the other hand was more complicated to discern because her story is a book inside of a book and the book is supposed to be deep. The problem is that I kept on imagining it as Darcy’s book so I had a different view of it while reading than I did after I finished the book and realized that it wasn’t Darcy’s book, it was Scott Westerfeld’s book. <strong>Lizzie’s storyline was written in a certain way to emphasize that it was Darcy’s book and we could see hints of Darcy’s personality in it</strong>. Basically, Scott Westerfeld is a genius.<strong> </strong>Are you guys starting to get the idea that I really love Scott? WELL IT’S BECAUSE HE’S A BLOODY GENIUS. Moving on. Lizzie’s character was similar in some ways to Darcy but at the same time, it wasn’t. Their situations were different and, in some ways, Lizzie was more mature. She made silly mistakes too but she dealt with things in a way I felt like Darcy probably wouldn’t have if she were in the same situation. It really was so interesting to compare and contrast the two and see how Scott created developed their characters.<br /><br /><strong>The world building in this book is magnificent.</strong> It’s hard to write a book about a book. Especially when the writer of the book is also a character (of the book that is being written about… are you confused yet?). <strong>But he does it and he does it well.</strong> I don’t really know how to explain it to y’all because it’s hard. Scott’s world consists of the bookish community but it also consists of the world he created for the book that is inside this book. The world building of <em>Afterworlds</em>, which is (also) the name of Darcy’s book, is marvelous. Not going to elaborate but think psychopomps, a little bit of hindu myth and the afterworlds. But going back to Darcy’s timeline, you get to also see the ways in which <em>Afterworlds</em> isn’t perfect. Y<strong>ou see criticism for the other timeline, something you might have been thinking yourself and that just makes this all the more interesting.</strong><br /><br />I am getting to the point of my review where I usually start to talk about the romances and you know what? I won’t. The romances don’t matter. I mean they do, but everything else that is going on in this book is so much bigger and better. I will offer one hint though;<strong> both romances have relevance.</strong><br /><br />I am not going to say anymore. I will shut up and all I ask of you is to give this a shot. Don’t be intimidated by the size, you’re going to love it and if not love it, at the very least, like it. It’s Scott Westerfeld after all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13813945941074235190" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1147644034" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1147644034" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2329228-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433629372p2/2329228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1147644034?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/2329228-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1147644034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5302993544222378317">Just a note, because I wish someone had told me before I began this book, it has a lesbian romance. That isn't the reason it got one star. Plenty of other reasons for that. Still. I wish I'd known going in. <br/><br/><i>Afterworlds</i> by Scott Westerfeld dripped with potential. At least initially. I'm a big fan of the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6050678.Leviathan__Leviathan___1_' title='Leviathan (Leviathan, #1) by Scott Westerfeld' rel='nofollow noopener'>Leviathan</a> trilogy. I liked <i>Midnighters</i>. The idea of a book intertwining a young author in New York City with corresponding chapters of her debut paranormal novel sounded like a must read. <br/>And init</span>
  <span id="freeText5302993544222378317" style="display:none">Just a note, because I wish someone had told me before I began this book, it has a lesbian romance. That isn't the reason it got one star. Plenty of other reasons for that. Still. I wish I'd known going in. <br /><br /><i>Afterworlds</i> by Scott Westerfeld dripped with potential. At least initially. I'm a big fan of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6050678.Leviathan__Leviathan___1_" title="Leviathan (Leviathan, #1) by Scott Westerfeld" rel="nofollow noopener">Leviathan</a> trilogy. I liked <i>Midnighters</i>. The idea of a book intertwining a young author in New York City with corresponding chapters of her debut paranormal novel sounded like a must read. <br />And initially, it was. <br />Darcy, the 18-year-old authoress, is Indian. Her book borrows characters from Hindu religions. Sweet. Less Greeks! More myth variation! I was all aboard. And then...the two intertwining stories threads fall apart. Internally. <br />Let's begin with Darcy's story. First off, she sends in a rough draft of her first-ever writing and not only gets accepted by a publisher, they role out the red carpet. Okay. That's part of the initial pull of the story, right? Yet for Darcy there doesn't appear to <i>be</i> anything she can't succeed at. She goes apartment hunting on a budget. Finds something. Can't afford the apartment? Gets her aunt to co-sponsor the loan. Gets writers block? Can't figure out her second novel? She'll deal with it and you know she will because the book has to end at some point. Throw in some irritating misunderstandings and relationship drama and you have a completely uninspiring, uninteresting plot about a girl who practically gets everything, is <i>told</i> she gets everything, and doesn't learn anything painful along the way. <br />Frequent language. Lots of f-bombs. <br />However, for me I grew bored the minute Darcy got a girlfriend. A girlfriend 5 years older than her. They all but move in together right away. <br />Let's review, shall we?<br />Darcy grew in an apparently traditional, possibly conservative Hindu home. She's never been in a relationship. She <i>just</i> turned 18. she graduates from high school and that summer moves to NYC. <br />Now, Imogen (said girlfriend) is 23 and has had previous girlfriends. Imogen is all like "I like you." And Darcy, who apparently hadn't a clue about her previous personal lesbian tendencies, is all like...smooch. <br />So goes young adult fiction.<br />Yet even young adult fiction cannot get me to believe or accept that a girl like Darcy, at her age and life experience raised as she was, would within...what? a week? be sleeping with her girlfriend. It sort of gets shuffled around with what it means to lose your virginity, bleh bleh bleh. No biggie, aw how sweet her first. Everyone is totally chill. Parents, friends, etc.<br />Um, how about no? <br />How about we reverse genders a little here? Would it make sense for an 18 year old girl who has never been in a relationship to be sleeping with a 23 year old boyfriend in such a short amount of time? I feel like we'd be a little concerned there. Why is it okay if its another female? No specifics offered - <i>thankfully</i> - but still completely bizarre, out of character, and stupid. Their relationship drama bored me to tears. <br />I ended up skimming through most of Darcy's plot. I couldn't stand her, her relationship, or her life. <br />Now Lizzie's story, the "novel" of this...um...novel, is about this girl who nearly dies in a terrorist attack and subsequently becomes a grim reaper/valkyrie/psychochamp ('psycho', you think? Wait till you hear that connection made....a million times...in this novel) And of course there is a nice, hot psychochamp dude who helps save her and somehow they have this burning connection. For the first half (or at least, longer then Darcy at any rate), Lizzie interested me. I really thought it had potential. The writing was off but I thought that was supposed to be Darcy's...rough draft? but no, its her final draft. <b>The rest of this paragraph will contain spoilers. </b>I felt the plot takes a random turn when Lizzie picks up Mindy's defense and goes to fight the 'bad man'. The terrorist just got dropped. The FBI sort of flits off the pages. Everything that began this book sort of gets ignored. And I didn't like it. The Mom's illness is out of left field. Its lousily paced. And frankly, it goes teeny bopper. I was left skimming Darcy's bit and reading a stupid, paranormal teen novel. What was the point? I'm not sure which plot line I wanted to end more. <br />Self-mockery and critique of teen novels in Darcy's bit doesn't make Lizzie's story any better. If anything it continues to drag it down.<br />Lots of potential, doesn't live up to it. If anything, avoid for all the swearing. Or for the lousy plots. Or thin characters. Or the writing. Or...you name it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5302993544222378317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1468086719?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley Daviau" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley Daviau" href="/user/show/35915119-ashley-daviau">Ashley Daviau</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>
