        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1468086719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4750028614808103615">I had a pretty good feeling I'd enjoy this book because I've very much enjoyed every other book by Westerfeld that I've read. But I didn't expect to fall head over heels in love with this book like I did! From the very beginning I was totally hooked and it only got better as it went on! <br/><br/>The concept and the way it's written is SO original and I really couldn't have enjoyed it more! I'm a huge YA fan so when I saw where this was going, I was so excited. Getting to read about Darcy going through th</span>
  <span id="freeText4750028614808103615" style="display:none">I had a pretty good feeling I'd enjoy this book because I've very much enjoyed every other book by Westerfeld that I've read. But I didn't expect to fall head over heels in love with this book like I did! From the very beginning I was totally hooked and it only got better as it went on! <br /><br />The concept and the way it's written is SO original and I really couldn't have enjoyed it more! I'm a huge YA fan so when I saw where this was going, I was so excited. Getting to read about Darcy going through the publishing process for her debut YA novel and then actually getting to read said debut YA novel was so damn awesome! <br /><br />Both sides of the story were absolutely excellent! Getting a "behind the scenes" look at the publishing world was really interesting and I did very much enjoy that half of the story. But I definitely enjoyed getting to read Darcy's novel more, especially since it's totally something I'd pick up myself! <br /><br />Really, I could go on and on about how much I loved this book! It's definitely made it into my top ten favourite books of 2017 so far, it's just that damn good! If there is truly a "YA Heaven" as joked about in this book, Scott Westerfeld has most definitely earned his place there! </span>

<div id="review_1036154391" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1036154391" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1036154391?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Proffitt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Proffitt" href="/user/show/1331641-jacob-proffitt">Jacob Proffitt</a>

          marked it as unfinished

<span id="freeTextContainer15628495828926617800">I think this is just a personality mismatch and since I realized that relatively early on (and quit reading), I'll refrain from assigning a star-score. The book is heavier than some textbooks I had in college and that made the reading awkward. I couldn't quite buy a new YA author making a six-figure advance on her first book, either. But the real roadblock to getting into the book was the two heroines. They both felt so passive, each waiting for things to happen to them, and/or what actions they</span>
  <span id="freeText15628495828926617800" style="display:none">I think this is just a personality mismatch and since I realized that relatively early on (and quit reading), I'll refrain from assigning a star-score. The book is heavier than some textbooks I had in college and that made the reading awkward. I couldn't quite buy a new YA author making a six-figure advance on her first book, either. But the real roadblock to getting into the book was the two heroines. They both felt so passive, each waiting for things to happen to them, and/or what actions they <em>did</em> take were motivated by impulses that were either external, mysterious, or unacknowledged. Add that each section would interrupt what little momentum I had from the other and you have a book I put down and simply couldn't be bothered to pick back up again.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1049948388" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1049948388" />
    <a title="Yzabel Ginsberg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/549584-yzabel-ginsberg"><img alt="Yzabel Ginsberg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442957782p2/549584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1049948388?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yzabel Ginsberg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yzabel Ginsberg" href="/user/show/549584-yzabel-ginsberg">Yzabel Ginsberg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21796007-afterworlds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=arc-netgalley">arc-netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=good-ideas-but">good-ideas-but</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=paranormal-supernatural">paranormal-supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=ghosts">ghosts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=tstl">tstl</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/549584-yzabel-ginsberg?shelf=annoying-characters">annoying-characters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1049948388" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6691653838209085695"><i>(I got an ARC courtesy of NetGalley, in exchange for an honest review.)</i><br/><br/>Pretty interesting premise, but in the end I found the execution wanting, and the stories not that interesting, unfortunately.<br/><br/>I really liked the beginning: Darcy having to navigate her way in New York, meeting published authors as well as other "debs" like her (people whose book was to be published in the upcoming months), having to take editing and rewriting tasks into account... The first pages of Lizzie's story were grippi</span>
  <span id="freeText6691653838209085695" style="display:none"><i>(I got an ARC courtesy of NetGalley, in exchange for an honest review.)</i><br /><br />Pretty interesting premise, but in the end I found the execution wanting, and the stories not that interesting, unfortunately.<br /><br />I really liked the beginning: Darcy having to navigate her way in New York, meeting published authors as well as other "debs" like her (people whose book was to be published in the upcoming months), having to take editing and rewriting tasks into account... The first pages of Lizzie's story were gripping, too, and I appreciated how we're shown the final version of Darcy's book, running parallel to her own editing of the first draft, with all the pitfalls that were in it (exposition chapters, huge info-dumps...) and were then removed. As someone who likes writing, too,<br />I couldn't help but find this comment about the YA scene and authors' jobs quite interesting. The book is full of little allusions to similar themes: Darcy obviously wrote her novel during NaNoWriMo 2012, the Darcy/Lizzie hint at "Pride and Prejudice" is totally acknowledged, the authors debate about what's more important (plot? characters? conflict? setting?), and so on.<br /><br />However, a lot of aspects in "Afterworlds" were problematic.<br /><br />For starters, I'm not sure YA readers not specifically interested in writing would "get it". Clearly it's going to be a hit-or-miss here.<br /><br />Also, the characters weren't that impressive. Those from Darcy's novel were rather bland in my opinion, and what I may deem "typical YA cut-outs". Yamaraj: the mysterious love interest without much of a personality. Jamie: the best friend who, in Darcy's copy-editor's own terms, "has car, lives with father", and not much more. In fact, Darcy's novel would have deserved to stand on its own, because it would've allowed the author to properly develop its world and characters, and make it the gripping idea it seemed to be in the beginning. (I'm still convinced that opening scene in the airport is a proper attention-catcher.)<br /><br />Darcy was definitely annoying: totally immature, without any sense of responsibility (she missed so many deadlines, such as the ones for college applications, lease renewal, and her writing was two inches from going the same way), jumping to conclusions, thinking in terms of the world revolving around her... Defects I would've happily forgiven, if she had learnt from them, but she didn't. And in the end? In the end, Little Miss Lucky still got lucky, still landed an astonishing deal, still managed to waltz out of problems without that much of a hitch. All things that are potential insults at actual writers, the large majority that doesn't land an agent after just a few weeks of querying, nor a $300,000 book deal for his/her first novel. I'm all in favour of selling dreams, but those were too much a matter of dumb luck, not of work and personal improvement. I didn't root for Darcy at all. (I was also rather miffed at her plot taking a "let's focus on the love relationship" turn. There were so many other things it could have focused on...)<br /><br />Mostly, I felt that this book had great potential in being a pretty good parody, but couldn't make up its mind about being one or not. Why a parody? For all the jabs at YA novels, at their shortcomings, elements I tend to notice as well when I read such stories. "Afterworlds" could be an excellent critique of the current market—a market I personally find saturated with cookie-cutter themes and plots (the same old kind of love interest, the same trend of characters whose questionable decisions put them in the too-stupid-to-live category...). Unfortunately, the way it is, it fell into the exact pit traps it (unconsciously or not?) denounced.<br /><br />A note as well about a few questions raised throughout Darcy's narrative. There was an interesting discussion about culture appropriation, and how Darcy's use of Yama, an actual deity from Hindu mythology, amounted to erasing Hinduism, or at least part of it, from her world, by not openly acknowledging him as part of this religion. I found this point very valid. And yet, at the same time, Darcy herself represents a removal of cultural heritage: she's of Indian origin, but apart from her surname and physical description, she's the typical "white protagonist". (She's not religious, her family isn't particularly religious either, they all behave like standard Americans in novels... In other words: why make her from a different culture, if it's not to use it? Was it just for the sake of having a non-white protagonist... or, on the contrary, to point at how many other novels appropriate various cultures, only to "whiten" them?)<br /><br />The underlying critique is definitely present, and something I can't help thinking about, wondering if it <i>was</i> on purpose, or totally accidental. I don't know how to take this novel, except with a grain of salt. I'm giving it 2 stars because of the parody it could be, one that made me snicker and nod my head in acknowledgment. But story-wise, I think it should either have been made a stronger read (as it was, it became boring rather quickly), or have gone all the way as a more obvious means of denouncing the many problems going rampant in the YA publishing industry. If it's one, I'm not sure that many people will realise it, unfortunately (and especially not younger readers—not because they're young, just because they may not have the necessary reading background to see the critique I mentioned).</span>
  <a data-text-id="6691653838209085695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1944668079" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1944668079" />
    <a title="catherine ♡" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48622339-catherine"><img alt="catherine ♡" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528240256p2/48622339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1944668079?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="catherine ♡" class="user" itemprop="url" name="catherine ♡" href="/user/show/48622339-catherine">catherine ♡</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48622339-catherine?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48622339-catherine?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48622339-catherine?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48622339-catherine?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1944668079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18190297692554192699"><b>Actual Rating:</b> 3.5<br/><br/>I've read Scott Westerfeld's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24770.Uglies__Uglies___1_' title='Uglies (Uglies, #1) by Scott Westerfeld' rel='nofollow noopener'>Uglies</a> series, but it hadn't been as good as I had expected with all the hype around it. Now, <i>Afterworlds</i> didn't have nearly as much exposure, but I loved it a lot better.<br/><br/>It is written from two perspectives:<br/>Darcy is an eighteen-year-old debut novelist who is waiting for her book <i>Afterworlds</i> to be published. She moves to New York to find a place where she can write, but there there, she finds love - and a little bit of herself.<br/>Our second story is the</span>
  <span id="freeText18190297692554192699" style="display:none"><b>Actual Rating:</b> 3.5<br /><br />I've read Scott Westerfeld's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24770.Uglies__Uglies___1_" title="Uglies (Uglies, #1) by Scott Westerfeld" rel="nofollow noopener">Uglies</a> series, but it hadn't been as good as I had expected with all the hype around it. Now, <i>Afterworlds</i> didn't have nearly as much exposure, but I loved it a lot better.<br /><br />It is written from two perspectives:<br />Darcy is an eighteen-year-old debut novelist who is waiting for her book <i>Afterworlds</i> to be published. She moves to New York to find a place where she can write, but there there, she finds love - and a little bit of herself.<br />Our second story is the story written by Darcy - it is about Lizzie, a girl who, after surviving a terrorist attack, finds out she has the ability to cross into the afterworld. But not all people are good, not all ghosts are good, and Lizzie's gonna need a little help learning what it means to believe.<br /><br />I thought the voices for the characters were done very well for this book. I'm not a huge fan of multiple perspectives because it's oftentimes so difficult to differentiate between them, but Lizzie and Darcy were in such different worlds that it was very clear. (The different page styles did help too.) Admittedly, I was more interested in Lizzie's story just because there was a much more sense of fantasy and adventure, whereas Darcy's was dimmed down and mundane. Both characters definitely undergone character development, but I felt like Darcy's character arc was more fluid.<br /><br />The plot for both were very interesting, but I felt like I liked the first half of the book more, for both. Lizzie's beginning was interesting, and I loved reading about how she ventured in the different worlds. There was great backstory about the ghosts that she met, and Westerfeld was great at the world-building. I feel like, in the second half, the story started to get a little messy, and I wasn't really sure where the story was headed anymore.<br /><br />Darcy had development as well, but it was very closely tied with her journey as an author, which, while realistic, was a bit more boring. There were times where I felt like she was annoying and hypocritical, which added to the fact that I didn't like her as much despite her character progression.<br /><br />The writing style was engaging; this was what had made me dislike <i>Uglies</i>, and it was why I entered this book with such hesitance, but the writing was surprisingly interesting.<br /><br />One issue I have with this is the love interest; Darcy's was great, but Lizzie's was such a cringeworthy case of insta-love that I would have honestly preferred it to not be there at all. This was perhaps the only reason my actual rating for the book was a 3.5, not a 4.<br /><br />Overall, this book actually reinstated my interest in Scott Westerfeld's other books, and I'd recommend <i>Afterworlds</i> to anyone into the fantasy, contemporary, and YA genres.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18190297692554192699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13759465219022742361">Different. Interesting. Meta.<br/><br/><b>Yay:</b> I loved both stories. I loved the meta-ness of Darcy's story, reading about her experiences and her rewrite struggles even as I read the final version of the story she had written.<br/><b>Nay:</b> Darcy has very few obstacles to her success, which is a little strange. I feel like that works into the Meta-ness, and I'm sure if I read closer on a reread at some point I can look closer at that, but it'll be awhile before I reread.<br/><br/><b>Yay:</b> Darcy and Imogen are super cute, even if t</span>
  <span id="freeText13759465219022742361" style="display:none">Different. Interesting. Meta.<br /><br /><b>Yay:</b> I loved both stories. I loved the meta-ness of Darcy's story, reading about her experiences and her rewrite struggles even as I read the final version of the story she had written.<br /><b>Nay:</b> Darcy has very few obstacles to her success, which is a little strange. I feel like that works into the Meta-ness, and I'm sure if I read closer on a reread at some point I can look closer at that, but it'll be awhile before I reread.<br /><br /><b>Yay:</b> Darcy and Imogen are super cute, even if they have slightly frustrating arguments (this is realistic, so I can't complain).<br /><b>Nay:</b> The development of their relationship...wasn't. It just happened. Like, from acquaintances to kissing in no time at all. Very strange. And while I like them as a couple, I would have loved to see the development instead of the insta.<br /><br /><b>Yay:</b> I love Darcy's novel! It's so cool! And creepy. And different and weird and I really did enjoy it.<br /><b>Nay:</b> Not buying Yamaraj and Lizzie. And the first time we see Mindy was really weirdly written.<br /><br /><b>Yay:</b> Eighteen year old making super bad decisions! Particularly with regards to money...which I definitely buy, I wasn't as bad as Darcy but I wasn't the smartest person when I was eighteen. Also, with that much money? Given to an eighteen year old? Yeah I'm not surprised she went a little overboard.<br /><b>Nay:</b> She never really seems to have anything bad happen because of her poor money management. Again, this might factor into the meta, or it might play in if Westerfeld is planning a sequel, who knows! And by "who" I mean "Westerfeld"<br /><br />All in all, I really liked this. Not perfect, but different and interesting and very, very Meta.</span>

<div id="review_1487928172" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1487928172" />
    <a title="Faith M ✨" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29390149-faith-m"><img alt="Faith M ✨" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587515873p2/29390149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1487928172?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faith M ✨" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faith M ✨" href="/user/show/29390149-faith-m">Faith M ✨</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=unique-format">unique-format</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=dnf-try-again-later">dnf-try-again-later</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29390149-faith-m?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1487928172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8758631105825693665">DNF'd at 48%<br/><br/>This had a lot of potential to be a really unique and profound story. It's told in alternating chapters: one of Darcy, the writer finding her way through the world of Young Adult literature in New York City as a teenage debut author; the other is of Lizzie, the main character in Darcy's urban fantasy book, who is the sole survivor of a terrorist attack at an airport and subsequently becomes a psychopomp and falls in love with the Hindu death good Yamaraj.<br/><br/>Unfortunately, it was boring </span>
  <span id="freeText8758631105825693665" style="display:none">DNF'd at 48%<br /><br />This had a lot of potential to be a really unique and profound story. It's told in alternating chapters: one of Darcy, the writer finding her way through the world of Young Adult literature in New York City as a teenage debut author; the other is of Lizzie, the main character in Darcy's urban fantasy book, who is the sole survivor of a terrorist attack at an airport and subsequently becomes a psychopomp and falls in love with the Hindu death good Yamaraj.<br /><br />Unfortunately, it was boring af. Here's a list of problems:<br /><br />- Darcy expects the world to be handed to her on a silver platter. She makes a lot of mistakes but never faces any consequences.<br /><br />- Lizzie is the sole survivor of an American terrorist attack in an airport but 1) never experiences any PTSD or symptoms of trauma, 2) overcomes her trauma because she insta-loves Yamaraj in the first chapter when he kisses her even though he told her to forget about him and never come back, and 3) she's a freaking Mary Sue.<br /><br />- The terrorist attack could have been a really profound part of the story, being post 9/11 but instead it was just the method of her meet cute with Yama. It could have shown the complexities of terrorism, fear mongering, and islamaphobia in America today, but it was just an excuse for Yamaraj to kiss her into another dimension.<br /><br />- Darcy's story, well generally less stupid, is just plain boring. I'm not super into contemporary unless someone's dead or about to be, so I was out of my element.<br /><br />- Given how cliché and illogical the book in the book is, why the actual f was it acclaimed in universe as being astounding, having "the juice" and worth thousands of dollars? It's post-Twilight trash. It's generic and soulless. Even Darcy knows (is that supposed to be meta or bad writing?)<br /><br />- Lizzie lies to everyone all the time. She lies to her mother about experiencing PTSD. She lies to her best friend about Yamaraj and says instead that she's dating the FBI agent who's in charge of her security (at least she tells him about it). She lies to Yamaraj about exploring the Stranger Things Upside Down—I mean the flip side.<br /><br />- Can Scott Westerfeld stop having teenagers say "[adjective]-making"?? No one talks like that!!!<br /><br />- Also, goosepimples or whatever. Just say goosebumps like normal people.<br /><br />And here are some stuff I actually quite liked:<br /><br />- Darcy is Indian, which is unforgivably uncommon in today's YA literature, given that a significant part of the world's population is Indian. She's also queer, even though I don't like that her girlfriend is a lot older than her (that just makes me uncomfortable)<br /><br />- Idr her name for some reason, but the little ghost girl who follows Lizzie around (my brain wants to say Mindy, but I'm not totally sure) is actually a pretty great character and added much needed depth to the story. She's paranoid that the man who kidnapped and killed her, burying her in her own backyard, is still out there and will find her when he dies. Like that's some intense stuff!!<br /><br />- I liked the psychopomp stuff. I'm writing a psychopomp novel so it was cool for me<br /><br />- I appreciated the guide through the world of authors, but it felt unrealistic tbh<br /><br />Lol half of these have negative comments in them.<br /><br />I might retry at some point to see if any of these are resolved by the ending, but honestly I kinda don't care.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8758631105825693665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8982457988360873367">Afterworlds is 2 stories.<br/>The 1st story is about Darcy, who in her senior year of high school took the month of November to write a novel titled Afterworlds (sound familiar?).<br/>She gets a 2-novel book deal with a huge advance, and persuades her family to let her live in New York while she edits Afterworlds and writes the 2nd novel.<br/><br/>The 2nd story is Afterworlds, the novel Darcy has written.<br/>Afterworlds is a supernatural thriller about Lizzie, who discovers that she can slip into the space between life</span>
  <span id="freeText8982457988360873367" style="display:none">Afterworlds is 2 stories.<br />The 1st story is about Darcy, who in her senior year of high school took the month of November to write a novel titled Afterworlds (sound familiar?).<br />She gets a 2-novel book deal with a huge advance, and persuades her family to let her live in New York while she edits Afterworlds and writes the 2nd novel.<br /><br />The 2nd story is Afterworlds, the novel Darcy has written.<br />Afterworlds is a supernatural thriller about Lizzie, who discovers that she can slip into the space between life and death. <br /><br />I really liked this book. I liked Darcy's story, because I felt like it was a glimpse into the real-life adventures of a novelist on her path to getting her first novel published.<br />But I thought Lizzie's story was more interesting.<br /><br />This book really shows how creative the author is.<br />In one section of the book, we read the opening paragraph of another character's novel, and it was just as compelling and interesting as the 2 stories we already have in this book.<br /><br />In another chapter, 2 published authors and Darcy are giving an author event at a high school, and they are asked which is most important: plot, characters or conflict.<br />The 1st author tells a short story with a cliffhanging ending to emphasize that he thinks plot is most important.<br />And again, this short story draws us in -- I wanted more!<br /><br />I really enjoyed these short insights into the author's creative mind.<br />But overall, the book was just okay.<br /><br />Here's a link to my <a href="http://bit.ly/2avokZK" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">YouTube review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_932433207" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/932433207" />
    <a title="Robin Henry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26571333-robin-henry"><img alt="Robin Henry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603581659p2/26571333.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/932433207?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin Henry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin Henry" href="/user/show/26571333-robin-henry">Robin Henry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26571333-robin-henry?shelf=spoiler-alert">spoiler-alert</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer932433207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer947986528594008317">I have been a Westerfeld fan since before he was famous, so it was with great anticipation that I downloaded the e-galley and began reading his latest offering, Afterworlds. It was with great disappointment that I forced myself to finish reading it. Unlike earlier efforts such as the Uglies series or his steampunk series, Leviathan, or even the Midnighters, Afterworlds was a huge let down. It could be because my expectations were high. After all, Westerfeld had trained me to expect interesting s</span>
  <span id="freeText947986528594008317" style="display:none">I have been a Westerfeld fan since before he was famous, so it was with great anticipation that I downloaded the e-galley and began reading his latest offering, Afterworlds. It was with great disappointment that I forced myself to finish reading it. Unlike earlier efforts such as the Uglies series or his steampunk series, Leviathan, or even the Midnighters, Afterworlds was a huge let down. It could be because my expectations were high. After all, Westerfeld had trained me to expect interesting social commentary and big ideas worthy of discussion along with solid writing in his earlier work. Alas, though the writing remains solid, the subject matter turned to the pedestrian with Afterworlds. This new novel tells a dual story—one of a teen author, Darcy Patel, and the other is the novel Patel has written. Sections alternate between these two narratives. In both stories, Westerfeld embraces the trend of the moment and delivers tired yet trendy fodder for teen readers. Ultimate wish fulfillment—teen writes best seller, gets huge advance and finds the love of her life, who is of the same sex and there are absolutely no members of her fairly traditional Indian family who so much as raise an eyebrow at this. Much of Darcy’s story will be incomprehensible to average teen readers; it seems to be written with librarians, prize committees, and other writers in mind. What teenager in this universe collects first editions and has reading copies, or even knows what those are? Let’s check off the trends. Paranormal romance—check. Lesbian love—check. Extravagant use of the f-bomb—check. It seems that teens and twenty-somethings cannot have a conversation without inserting it, multiple times. That is, if we are to believe current writers, who for my money, are just too lazy to come up with something more meaningful in the way of dialog. <br />I found myself hoping that maybe this novel is actually a parody of the current state of YA literature. I am still hoping someone will tell me it is. If it turns out not to be a parody, I will approach Mr. Westerfeld’s next opus with much more caution.<br /><br />June’s Rating System: Language—R; Nudity—PG; Sexual Content—R; GLBT Content—R; Violence—R The opening scene of Darcy’s YA novel is a terrorist attack which takes place at an airport. Very bloody, somewhat graphic. Later scenes in the Underworld are not as violent, but there is a sinister theme. The main character of Darcy’s novel commits a murder for revenge, for which there are no consequences and no real questioning from a moral standpoint. Apparently, being a bad person means it is okay to murder you. Adult Themes—R, death violence, revenge, spiritual questions. The story of Darcy’s novel borrows from Hindu religious themes, which may offend observant Hindus.<br /><br />Robin’s Comments: Disappointed just isn’t a strong enough word for this. I did not find that the novel had enough literary merit to make up for the baggage. I would definitely not recommend this for students younger than ninth grade.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="947986528594008317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_952736756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/952736756" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8769426-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430689841p2/8769426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/952736756?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/8769426-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8769426-ashley?shelf=physical-arc">physical-arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8769426-ashley?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8769426-ashley?shelf=bea14">bea14</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer952736756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14457742543281512133"><b><a href='http://www.nosegraze.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nose Graze</a> — Book reviews & blogging tips</b><br/><br/> Let's clarify some terms <br/><br/>Before we begin, I need to set down some terminology for my review. As you probably know, the book <em>
  <strong>Afterworlds</strong>
</em> by Scott Westerfeld is two books in one, told in alternating chapters. One story is that of Darcy, a soon-to-be-published YA author. The story is about her selling her book, doing rewrites, and drafting the next book in the series. The other story is about Darcy's book. We actually get to read it. However, just to co</span>
  <span id="freeText14457742543281512133" style="display:none"><b><a href="http://www.nosegraze.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Nose Graze</a> — Book reviews &amp; blogging tips</b><br /><br /> Let's clarify some terms <br /><br />Before we begin, I need to set down some terminology for my review. As you probably know, the book <em>
  <strong>Afterworlds</strong>
</em> by Scott Westerfeld is two books in one, told in alternating chapters. One story is that of Darcy, a soon-to-be-published YA author. The story is about her selling her book, doing rewrites, and drafting the next book in the series. The other story is about Darcy's book. We actually get to read it. However, just to confuse things, that 'story within the story' is also called Afterworlds.<br /><br />From this point on out, please abide by the following definitions:<br /><br /> 
  <br />
   <strong>This book</strong> — Refers to the 'real' published book <em>Afterworlds</em> by Scott Westerfeld. 
  <br />
   <strong>Afterworlds</strong> — Refers to the 'story within the story', which is the YA paranormal book that Darcy writes. 
  <br />
 <br /><br /> Uggghh! <br /><br />This book had a lot of potential. For the most part (except towards the end), I liked the contemporary, YA author story. It was cool to watch an author go through the world of publishing. But, for the most part, I hated the story she was writing (Afterworlds).<br /><br /> The paranormal ghost story (Afterworlds) <br /><br />I hated it. Every single time I got to one of these chapters, I asked myself the same question:<br /><br /><blockquote>Can I skip this chapter? Will the book still make sense if I don't read the whole 'Afterworlds' part of the book?</blockquote><br /><br />I never did skip a chapter, but much to my dismay, I could have. Now, looking back, I'm screaming at myself for NOT skipping because IT WAS POINTLESS! Even at the end, the Afterworlds part of the book never actually felt important. Everything still would have made sense if that part of the book was stripped out.<br /><br />But anyway...<br /><br /> I don't like ghost stories <br /><br />To be fair, I never gave the Afterworlds part of the book much of a chance. I did like the first chapter with the terrorist attack, but once it turned paranormal, I wasn't interested anymore. But, I think a large portion of that is on me: I don't like ghost stories. That's just a preference I have. Some people don't like sci-fi, some don't like contemporary, some don't like romance... I don't like ghost stories. And Afterworlds was <em>definitely</em> a ghost story.<br /><br /> Lamest Twilight-inspired romance ever <br /><br />But on top of that, it was also a romance... sort of. I got the idea that it was supposed to be a romance. Lizzie meets a "hot, shiny boy" and gets a bit gaga for him.<br /><br /><blockquote>Even in that awful moment, I could see that he was beautiful. He shone somehow, as if sunlight were breaking through the mist, just for him. He was one of those boys with a perfect jaw, who looks stunning when he's clean shaven, but just that little bit more handsome with the barest shadow of stubble.<br /><cite>Afterworlds, page 24</cite></blockquote><br /><br />They never really have a "we are now dating" or "will you go out with me?" moment; they just kiss a few times and then suddenly that's the norm. It was horrible! It was so insta-lovey, but without any actual love. It was more like "insta-kiss-and-now-our-lips-are-glued-together-but-we-aren't-in-love". How's that for confusing?<br /><br /><blockquote>His lips pressed against mine, and a wave of heat floored into me.<br /><cite>Afterworlds, page 31</cite></blockquote><br /><br />Look at that: first kiss on page 31. And that's <em>after</em> like two chapters of the <em>contemporary</em> story (so more like page 15).<br /><br /> Is there a personality in there? <br /><br />But for me, I think the worst part of this Lizzie/Yamaraj romance was the fact that YAMARAJ HAD NO PERSONALITY!!! The guy was such a polite, faceless blank slate. He had no emotion, no personality, nothing! He just existed, spoke a few words now and then, and sparkled like a Twilight vampire.<br /><br /> The contemporary "YA Author" story <br /><br />I started out quite enjoying the "YA author on the rise" part of this story. I thought it was really cool to see a young author enter the world of publishing. I loved reading about the "YA drink night" she was invited to, receiving the notes from her editor, being on deadline. I have no idea how accurate it was, being that I'm not an author myself, but it was fun to read about nonetheless.<br /><br />Then a romance kicked in.. out of NOWHERE! I didn't see it coming. There was no build up, no flirting, no hints, nada! Then it just slammed into the story full force. For a while, that turned me off the contemporary part of the story because at first there was just no chemistry; it was so insta-lovey (again). Eventually it did win me back over.. for a while.<br /><br /> The fact that I hated the paranormal story weakened the book overall <br /><br />Really disliking the paranormal "story within the story" made me like the "YA author" story even less. The YA author part was about this girl writing, selling, and publishing her book <em>Afterworlds</em>. At first I loved this because it was so interesting to read about an author's journey through the publishing world. But as the book went on and as I read more of <em>Afterworlds</em>, I began to hate <em>Afterworlds</em> more and more. That made it almost awkward to read the contemporary part of the story. How could I be excited with Darcy if I hated her book? Had I actually picked up her version of <em>Afterworlds</em> at BEA (without the contemporary YA author chapters), I would have DNF'd it. It almost made me laugh that she sold her duology for $300,000 and it was a freaking shit book.<br /><br />Then as the book came to its final 100 or 200 pages, I realized two things:<br /><br /> 
  <br />
   1. Sometimes it felt like the romance had a bigger focus than the writing/author part of the story, and that annoyed me. 
  <br />
   2. Darcy was young and immature.. and that annoyed me even more. 
  <br />
 <br /><br /> Why did the romance have to take over? <br /><br />This is rare for me to actually say, but I was pissed that the romance started taking over. The big, central conflict of the story wasn't really about writing or publishing; it was about the romance having a hitch. Ugh.<br /><br /> Darcy is too young and careless to be living on her own <br /><br />And Darcy started a pathetic spiral down into "idiot" territory. At the beginning of the book, her sister gave her a budget. She had $100,000 from her advance and two years to live in New York. Her sister said: spend $x on rent and $x per day on living (food, etc.). Well, Darcy did the following:<br /><br /> 
  <br />
   Got an apartment out of her price range (like $1k per month over or something) 
  <br />
   Ate out at expensive restaurants like EVERY FREAKING NIGHT. So she went waaay over her "spend $x per day on living" budget. Seriously, have you heard of cooking? 
  <br />
   Missed her writing deadlines and begged for extensions. 
  <br />
   Missed her uni deadlines. 
  <br />
   Missed her lease renewal deadline. 
  <br />
 <br /><br />Seriously, is she trying to prove to the world that she's too young to start a career and live on her own? Her lack of common sense and independence started to really piss me off.<br /><br /> Give me another book about the life of an author <br /><br />I absolutely LOVE that idea for a book, and there were certainly bits in this book that I enjoyed. It was a lot of fun to read about the writing process, meeting (or missing) deadlines, having ARCs for BookExpo America, and so on. But I was disappointed to see the romance take over that awesome part of the book. And even more so, I was disappointed to find that half the book was both boring as shit and completely unnecessary.<br /><br /> <br /><br />Review originally posted on <a href="http://www.nosegraze.com/review-afterworlds-by-scott-westerfeld/" rel="nofollow noopener">Nose Graze</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14457742543281512133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1071982715" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1071982715" />
    <a title="Eilonwy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17237503-eilonwy"><img alt="Eilonwy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506803497p2/17237503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1071982715?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eilonwy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eilonwy" href="/user/show/17237503-eilonwy">Eilonwy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=diverse-characters">diverse-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=well-written">well-written</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=enjoyable-enough">enjoyable-enough</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=a-bit-on-the-long-side">a-bit-on-the-long-side</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1071982715" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9039835703649189056"><br/>Three and a quarter stars? Three and a half? <br/><br/><blockquote>Darcy Patel has won the writing jackpot: $300,000 for a two-book contract for her NaNoWriMo novel, Afterworlds, written during her senior year of high school. So instead of going to college, upon graduation she moves to New York, determined to live the glamorous lifestyle of the full-time author.<br/> <br/>Lizzie Scofield nearly dies during a terrorist attack at the Denver airport -- and discovers that she is a psychopomp, someone who can lead newly-dead souls </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9039835703649189056" style="display:none"><br />Three and a quarter stars? Three and a half? <br /><br /><blockquote>Darcy Patel has won the writing jackpot: $300,000 for a two-book contract for her NaNoWriMo novel, Afterworlds, written during her senior year of high school. So instead of going to college, upon graduation she moves to New York, determined to live the glamorous lifestyle of the full-time author.<br /> <br />Lizzie Scofield nearly dies during a terrorist attack at the Denver airport -- and discovers that she is a psychopomp, someone who can lead newly-dead souls to their proper afterworld. But the "flipside" is a tricky place, occupied not only by the handsome and helpful Yama, but also by other spirit guides who have their own agendas. </blockquote>The catch-phrase on the cover of this book is "Darcy writes the words. Lizzie lives them." Readers get both the story of Darcy's first year in NYC, and the published version of her novel. So it's two! two! two books in one. And wow, does it feel like it. It's 600 pages long, and weighs 2-1/2 pounds (over a kilogram!). Every other chapter alternates between Darcy and Lizzie, which seemed interesting to me at first, but by the end was just distracting. Someday if I decide to reread this, I'm going to read each book separately.<br /> <br />This book is so meta that I don't even know where to begin in trying to describe it. (Crickets have been chirping for a while as I try to get past that sentence.) Scott Westerfeld has created a volume that's determined to please everyone -- readers who love meaty yet unresolved contemporary stories, and readers who crave the action and worldbuilding of genre novels.<br /> <br />Darcy's story is a sort of insider love letter to the YA publishing industry and its authors. There's a John Green character. There's a guy named "Coleman Gayle." There's lots of talk about agents, and sales, and revisions, and writerly insecurities. There's discussion of cultural appropriation and diversity and genre. As a writer, I found most of this both entertaining and fascinating. But as a reader looking for a plot? Maybe not so much. There's some mild tension as Darcy frets about what she can possibly write the promised sequel about, worries about telling her family she's fallen in love with a woman, and goes way, way over her budget. But honestly, this stuff all felt kind of adult to me. Darcy might as well have just gotten out of college, not high school.<br /> <br />I found some fail as a writer, too. The first chapter TELLS all about Darcy's query letter to an agent, but doesn't show it. Why not? It's revealed pretty quickly that Darcy got this huge advance for the rough draft of her novel -- and before she even starts the revisions, everyone who has seen her book raves to her about what great writing she does and how fantastic the novel is. I just can't believe this. Nobody's first drafts are that good.<br /> <br />Which brings me to the Lizzie part of the book. It's a sort of paranormal romance/ghost story with a few revenge, suspense, and horror elements mixed in. It started off very strongly, only to meander a bit, lose some focus, and end on a bit of a whimper -- which I think may have been deliberate, seeing how many YA paranormal novels do just that. I did really enjoy this part of the story, although (a) I do not believe it for one second as having been written by a 17/18-year-old, and (b) I'm not sure how it would seem alone, and not as this living thing tied into the events in Darcy's story.<br /> <br />So, tying this all together, I'm not at all sure how I feel about this book. On the good side, Scott Westerfeld is a terrific writer, and I really enjoyed both parts of this book (plus one chapter from Darcy's girlfriend's book) as I was reading. On the not-so-good side, I don't know what to make of it all as a whole. It all started out strongly and excitingly, but by the end both stories felt a little awkward and unsatisfying.<br /> <br />I definitely recommend this book to anyone who writes and aspires to be part of the traditional YA publishing world. But I'm a little mystified as to who else this book was written for. I can't tell if it would have held my interest if I weren't a writer and following the YA scene fairly closely.<br /> <br />If you're at all curious, give it a shot! It's definitely made me want to read some other Scott Westerfeld books. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9039835703649189056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_883902663" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/883902663" />
    <a title="Kribu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5117138-kribu"><img alt="Kribu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307111631p2/5117138.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/883902663?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kribu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kribu" href="/user/show/5117138-kribu">Kribu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781481422369" href="/book/show/21943375-afterworlds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117138-kribu?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117138-kribu?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117138-kribu?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117138-kribu?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117138-kribu?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117138-kribu?shelf=received-for-review">received-for-review</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer883902663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3286748877945890479">I remember I was quite excited when I first heard about this book. I've enjoyed everything by Scott Westerfeld that I've read, and the concept for <i>Afterworlds</i> sounded ... different, and potentially very interesting indeed.<br/><br/>And then, when I had the book, I just... kept putting it off. It didn't feel right. I'm very much a mood reader, and I know myself well enough by now that if I'm in the wrong mood for a book, I'll end up not enjoying it in the least. So I kept putting it off, week after week.<br/><br/>We</span>
  <span id="freeText3286748877945890479" style="display:none">I remember I was quite excited when I first heard about this book. I've enjoyed everything by Scott Westerfeld that I've read, and the concept for <i>Afterworlds</i> sounded ... different, and potentially very interesting indeed.<br /><br />And then, when I had the book, I just... kept putting it off. It didn't feel right. I'm very much a mood reader, and I know myself well enough by now that if I'm in the wrong mood for a book, I'll end up not enjoying it in the least. So I kept putting it off, week after week.<br /><br />Well. I've read it now. And it took... a lot longer than it should have.<br /><br />Hence the three stars. Three and a half, I suppose - the last third or so, I liked quite a lot.<br /><br />The very first bit, too.<br /><br />It's that part in the middle that I had trouble with. It's still an interesting concept, as I had hoped, but somehow... I'm not sure, really. I loved the idea of a book that's half set in the YA publishing world, with bloggers and agents and editors and ARCs and signings and tours, and in the end, it worked well enough.<br /><br />For quite some time though it was just... a bit <i>too</i> meta. A bit <i>too</i> fourth wall breaking. A bit <i>too</i> knowing, and nodding, to the real world. A bit too carefully constructed, for example not only with an eye on diversity but the way the issues of diversity and cultural appropriation were handled in the book. <br /><br />At times, I felt like reading a social justice blog or tumblr. Not exactly a bad thing, and I applaud the idea, since these are issues that need to be discussed, but ... as I said, it felt too meta. It took me a long time to feel immersed in <i>Afterworlds</i>, both Darcy's story and Lizzie's story, and to stop feeling like I was reading blogs or articles instead.<br /><br />In other words, I suddenly wasn't at all sure if the concept itself worked for me - and it didn't help that for quite some time, Lizzie's story - the book <i>Afterworlds</i> within <i>Afterworlds</i> - didn't engage me either. And then both Lizzie's story and Darcy's story became, for a while, too focused on all the various kissing, none of which appealed to me or interested me, because I didn't feel anything whatsoever for Yama and Imogen bored me as well. <br /><br />It got better, though. I don't know when exactly, or how, but at some point, about two thirds into the book, it took on a life of its own, leaving the feeling of meta behind, and I finally felt interested in the protagonists of both stories. Their lives started feeling <i>real</i>, and I was intrigued at last. So in that sense, I suppose I should say it did, in the end, work for me.<br /><br />It's definitely a book worth reading, especially for anyone who doesn't mind such a somewhat more experimental structure and concept, and it certainly gives some interesting insight into the world of publishing.<br /><br /><i>* ARC of this book was provided by the publisher through NetGalley in return for an honest review. Thanks!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3286748877945890479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_959813759" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/959813759" />
    <a title="Dahlia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4688700-dahlia"><img alt="Dahlia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442886197p2/4688700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/959813759?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dahlia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dahlia" href="/user/show/4688700-dahlia">Dahlia</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=quiltbag-ya">quiltbag-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=racial-diversity-in-ya-na">racial-diversity-in-ya-na</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=f-f-romance">f-f-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=interracial-romance">interracial-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=qpoc">qpoc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=fave-romances">fave-romances</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=2014-releases">2014-releases</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer959813759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6727154220969624927">I am so, so curious who the intended audience was for this book. On the one hand, you have a wholly contemporary storyline about a YA writer who gets a huge book deal and moves to Manhattan (which is one of those things I'll never understand - why do people think they need to move to NYC to write? I live here and I don't get this) and falls in love with another YA writer. Then you have the other POV, which is wholly paranormal. <br/><br/>So, take me for instance. I am an author. I am a contemp YA and NA l</span>
  <span id="freeText6727154220969624927" style="display:none">I am so, so curious who the intended audience was for this book. On the one hand, you have a wholly contemporary storyline about a YA writer who gets a huge book deal and moves to Manhattan (which is one of those things I'll never understand - why do people think they need to move to NYC to write? I live here and I don't get this) and falls in love with another YA writer. Then you have the other POV, which is wholly paranormal. <br /><br />So, take me for instance. I am an author. I am a contemp YA and NA lover. I am particularly fond of LGBT romances. And I live in NYC. For me, the contemporary storyline was fantastic - I enjoyed the hell out of it, and all the familiar references and experiences and the romance itself. But I kept thinking, "What would I think of this if I *weren't* a writer? Would this be anywhere near as much fun if I weren't so conscious of how meta it is? This feels like it was written for me, but...current me. Would I have liked this in high school? Do teens like this?" And I'm still really curious about those answers.<br /><br />As for the paranormal chapters...well, while I ended up enjoying plenty about AFTERWORLDS by Scott Westerfeld, I actually DNFed AFTERWORLDS by Darcy Patel. Like, just straight up skipped those chapters after a while so I could keep reading the contemp. There just wasn't anything in them I found appealed to someone whose primary book of interest were exactly what Westerfeld had done in the Darcy chapters. <br /><br />As such, I can't really rate this, and I won't, but I will say this: If Westerfeld had done a contemp NA with just the Darcy chapters, and fleshed out the romance/first love/sexual orientation question some, since I think that stuff fell to the wayside in order to accommodate this book's already being a leviathan, I would've five-starred it in a heartbeat. And yes, NA, because in a world where all marketing categories were viewed as equal, that's exactly what this should've been - it's got nothing to do with the high-school-aged adolescent experience and everything to do with figuring out what comes next professionally and personally. Nothing about it really felt YA to me, and it made me feel like this isn't going to find the right readers. But then again, as the ARC loudly declared on the back, they're spending a crapton of money to market it, so WTF do I know. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6727154220969624927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1466552494" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1466552494" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11561272-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569597647p2/11561272.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1466552494?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/11561272-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11561272-jenna?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1466552494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5045351855778378702">The main reason I picked up this book was because it's always listed as having asexual (specifically demisexual) representation. As someone who identifies as asexual (and has considered being demisexual), I figured I should read it. So I did. But I didn't actually know the plot of the book going into it...then again...I often just forget to read summaries going into books. Oops.<br/><br/>Anyways, while I really enjoyed a lot of this book, I had a lot of problems with it too. My main issue was Lizzie's sto</span>
  <span id="freeText5045351855778378702" style="display:none">The main reason I picked up this book was because it's always listed as having asexual (specifically demisexual) representation. As someone who identifies as asexual (and has considered being demisexual), I figured I should read it. So I did. But I didn't actually know the plot of the book going into it...then again...I often just forget to read summaries going into books. Oops.<br /><br />Anyways, while I really enjoyed a lot of this book, I had a lot of problems with it too. My main issue was Lizzie's story aka all of Darcy's novel. It really read like a NaNo novel I would write myself, which I guess kind of matches Darcy's style and her character, but it was kind of frustrating to read knowing it was written by Scott Westerfeld, a legit published author. I kept getting annoyed with the plot and the characters in Darcy's story because it feels flat and poorly done and kind of lame, honestly. Also a little confusing. I guess it was cool because you did get to feel like you were in Darcy's world and you knew what she was working on, but I did not enjoy it much at all. The romance between Lizzie and Yama was super rushed and totally unbelievable. Yama honestly felt like this flat, undeveloped character who didn't do anything. (Which, could mean a number of things - women are objectified like this all the time in movies and literature, so it shouldn't feel so weird to me. Also she comments a lot that he's just this hot death god who doesn't really have any purpose so I guess that fits...) I couldn't stand Lizzie as a character and I really hated the world Darcy created. It made no sense and it was really kind of weird. <br /><br />All that aside, I ADORED Darcy's POV chapters. The few times she talks about her sexuality I did get a distinctly ace/demi vibe, so I really appreciated that. I loved how she was homoromantic and Indian at that! Going in no one told me it was such a diverse book, and I really appreciated that. Also all the Indian/Hindu religion references were super cool and unique. I also loved how this book tackled a lot of hard writing topics like borrowing other people's ideas and cultures and writing with white MCs so more people could relate to them. While the problems were never really solved or fully answered, they spurred up some great discussion and I think it made me consider a lot about diverse books. Also I LOVE LOVE LOVED the writing plot of this book - especially hearing about BEA. (Is it May yet?) The fact that Darcy did NaNo and got published was slightly unrealistic, but also really inspiring! It was so cool to see the publishing world laid out in a fiction book, and to see what it's really like to be a paid/published author. Since I'm finishing this Oct 27, making sure to finish it before Nov starts, I am HELLA PUMPED TO START MY NEXT NOVEL. I realized that this year's idea is actually loosely similar to Afterworlds (in that it's set in hell &amp; there's kind of a god of death) but in my head I'm like "OKAY I CAN WRITE THIS IDEA BETTER THAN DARCY" so it's like a challenge. <br /><br />Overall I loved half of this book desperately, but the rest of it left a lot to be desired. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5045351855778378702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1014242438" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1014242438" />
    <a title="Jill Williamson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/955038-jill-williamson"><img alt="Jill Williamson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442498975p2/955038.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1014242438?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill Williamson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill Williamson" href="/user/show/955038-jill-williamson">Jill Williamson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1014242438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4924038792280042757">I was really excited to read this book. I’m a big fan of Scott Westerfeld’s work. In Afterworlds we read two stories in one. The first is Darcy’s story. She is an eighteen-year-old girl who sold her first book to a New York publisher for a ton of money. And we follow her life in New York as she attends parties and tries to write the sequel. The second is Lizzie’s story. She is the protagonist of Darcy’s novel. She survives a terrorist attack and gains the ability to see the dead and travel to th</span>
  <span id="freeText4924038792280042757" style="display:none">I was really excited to read this book. I’m a big fan of Scott Westerfeld’s work. In Afterworlds we read two stories in one. The first is Darcy’s story. She is an eighteen-year-old girl who sold her first book to a New York publisher for a ton of money. And we follow her life in New York as she attends parties and tries to write the sequel. The second is Lizzie’s story. She is the protagonist of Darcy’s novel. She survives a terrorist attack and gains the ability to see the dead and travel to the Flipside, where she can talk to ghosts and interact with others like her.<br /><br />What I liked:<br /><br />I liked the concept. This type of story has been done before, but what hasn’t? I thought it was fun to go back and forth between Darcy’s life and the book she wrote.<br /><br />I liked the book she wrote. That story was intriguing and different. In hindsight, I kind of wish that would have been the whole book.<br /><br />What I didn’t like:<br /><br />I just didn’t care about Darcy. She was living this fairytale life that was completely unrealistic and just plain boring. I didn’t care that she couldn’t write because she didn’t ever sit down and try. I didn’t care about the relationship with her girlfriend because all she did was whine. Everything happened to Darcy. And everything conveniently worked out for Darcy without her having to do anything.<br /><br />Darcy’s publishing experience was unrealistic. I know many writers—many teen writers too—and no one gets it this easy. No one gets paid that much for a first book. And if it does happen, it’s a one-in-a-million type of thing. This book mocks the hard work of writing, the struggle that writers go through to get published, to deal with rejection, to want to quit, then deciding to persevere… It's an insult to hard-working teen writers. If you want to read a good book about a teen writer who gets published, check out The Revised Life of Ellie Sweet. That book tells it like it is.<br /><br />The book was preachy. Every character was the same, spoke the same (with the exception of Sagan, but even he thought the same as everyone else). Every character had the same quirky swearing, the same views on Darcy’s love life, the same worldview, the same robotically faithful attitude toward anything she did. It was patronizing. Life is full of conflict. People don’t agree on anything. I felt like the author was trying to push his views onto young readers. <br /><br />This book was such a disappointment. It almost felt like someone else wrote it and put his name on the cover. I do not recommend it. And I will be leery of what Westerfeld writes in the future. <br /><br />For parents and librarians, I rate this book R for language, violence, sexuality, and GLBT content. A main character commits premeditated murder and her only consequence is that she loses her boyfriend for a while. This was not a book I’d want my elementary school son, who so loved Leviathan, to read.<br /><br />*I received a free copy of this book from the Vine program in exchange for an honest review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4924038792280042757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/971329889" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer971329889" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer866314124386267564">I'm kind of torn over how I feel about this book - I liked some things about it and I disliked other things. I wasn't sure what to expect going into this because my friends on goodreads seem torn with half of them loving it and 5-staring it and the other half absolutely hating it. I think I'm somewhere in the middle. <br/><br/>This book is really two stories in one, which I thought was interesting. We get Darcy who is a YA author living in New York, and then we get Darcy's first YA paranormal novel "After</span>
  <span id="freeText866314124386267564" style="display:none">I'm kind of torn over how I feel about this book - I liked some things about it and I disliked other things. I wasn't sure what to expect going into this because my friends on goodreads seem torn with half of them loving it and 5-staring it and the other half absolutely hating it. I think I'm somewhere in the middle. <br /><br />This book is really two stories in one, which I thought was interesting. We get Darcy who is a YA author living in New York, and then we get Darcy's first YA paranormal novel "AfterWorlds" about a girl named Lizzie who can travel to the "afterworld". One thing I want to mention is that I appreciate the diversity in this book. The two love interests are an Indian man and a lesbian girl, so I thought it was cool to see so many diverse characters. I also loved the opening chapter of Afterworlds, where Lizzie is in the airport and there's a shooting. Her first experience of traveling to the afterworld was really cool and I was immediately intrigued.<br /><br />However, even though both stories were interesting and fun to read, you kind of have to suspend your belief when it comes to Darcy's story. I mean she's a teenager who got a huge book deal in the 100,000 range, and dropped everything and moved to New York. And this book is her first book ever, her first attempt at writing anything. And now she has a deal for a YA trilogy and is promised hundreds of thousands of dollars. It's just so unrealistic. The way her lifestyle as an author is portrayed is just so unrealistic and got so annoying to read about. If only it were that simple. I think most authors would be offended by this, knowing how much work goes into becoming an author and getting your start as a writer. It certainly doesn't just happen overnight the way it does for Darcy.<br /><br />The thing I like most about Lizzie's story in Afterworlds is the story about Lizzie's Mom's friend who got kidnapped as a little girl. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The man kidnapped her while her family was on a road trip and he buried the little girl in her families backyard. That is some creepy shit. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I didn't care much for the romance/insta-love between Lizzie and Yamaraj. <br /><br />At the end of the day I just thought both of the stories were pretty boring when I looked at the bigger picture. I was enjoying it somewhat as I was reading it, but then I would sit back and think about what I read and <i>nothing really happened.</i> Lizzie's story was a typical paranormal romance story and Darcy's chapters just involved writing her novel, talking about her budget and financial problems, and falling in love with Imogen. Also, this book is 600 pages and I think it's way too long for what it is. It could have easily been cut down by 200-300 pages. </span>
  <a data-text-id="866314124386267564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return

      Element.show('savingMessage')

      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name

Años después de que la censuraron y reprimieron por sacar a la luz el tema, cuando los poderes de la publicidad, el dinero y la hipocresía de las malas conciencias trataron de ocultar los crímenes de pederastia del fundador de la Legión de Cristo, Aristegui exhibe hoy, como un oportuno desquite, el coro de voces que siguen clamando por esa justicia aún pendiente. \nDesde el papa Juan Pablo II hasta el actual pontífice cuando se llamaba Ratzinger, los altos mandatarios de la jerarquía eclesiástica (que no la Iglesia del evangelio a la que pertenecemos los creyentes) son y han sido cómplices de este pecado social nunca asumido en su tiempo. Eso es lo que este libro muestra y demuestra merced a las charlas de Aristegui con víctimas y testigos justicieros. Un libro brutal.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13957.Scott_Westerfeld">About Scott Westerfeld</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13957.Scott_Westerfeld"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1535403091p3/13957.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18281019825196808095">Scott Westerfeld is a New York Times bestselling author of YA. He was born in the Texas and now lives in Sydney and New York City. In 2001, Westerfeld married fellow author Justine Larbalestier.<br/><br/>He is best known for the Uglies and Leviathan series. His next book, IMPOSTORS, returns to the world of Uglies. It comes out September 11, 2018.</span>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
